In 2007, readers of brandchannel.com voted Wikipedia as the fourth-highest brand ranking, receiving 15 percent of the votes in response to the question "Which brand had the most impact on our lives in 2006?"[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-brandchannel.com_awards_1-433)

In September 2008, Wikipedia received [Quadriga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadriga_(award) "Quadriga (award)")_A Mission of Enlightenment_ award of Werkstatt Deutschland along with [Boris Tadić](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Tadi%C4%87 "Boris Tadić"), [Eckart Höfling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eckart_H%C3%B6fling "Eckart Höfling"), and [Peter Gabriel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Gabriel "Peter Gabriel"). The award was presented to Wales by [David Weinberger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Weinberger "David Weinberger").[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-loomarea.com_WP_award_1-434)

In 2015, Wikipedia was awarded both the annual [Erasmus Prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erasmus_Prize "Erasmus Prize"), which recognizes exceptional contributions to culture, society or social sciences,[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-EP2015-435) and the Spanish [Princess of Asturias Award](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Princess_of_Asturias_Award "Princess of Asturias Award") on International Cooperation.[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-436) Speaking at the Asturian Parliament in Oviedo, the city that hosts the awards ceremony, [Jimmy Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jimmy_Wales "Jimmy Wales") praised the work of the [Asturian Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asturian_Wikipedia "Asturian Wikipedia") users.[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-437)

#### Satire

Comedian [Stephen Colbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Colbert "Stephen Colbert") has parodied or referenced Wikipedia on numerous episodes of his show _[The Colbert Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Colbert\_Report "The Colbert Report")_ and coined the related term _[wikiality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikiality "Wikiality")_, meaning "together we can create a reality that we all agree on—the reality we just agreed on".[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-wikiality-235) Another example can be found in "Wikipedia Celebrates 750 Years of American Independence", a July 2006 front-page article in _[The Onion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Onion "The Onion")_,[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-onion_WP_750_years_1-438) as well as the 2010 _The Onion_ article "'L.A. Law' Wikipedia Page Viewed 874 Times Today".[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-439)

In an April 2007 episode of the American television comedy _[The Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Office\_(American\_TV\_series) "The Office (American TV series)")_, office manager ([Michael Scott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Scott_(The_Office) "Michael Scott (The Office)")) is shown relying on a hypothetical Wikipedia article for information on [negotiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negotiation "Negotiation") tactics to assist him in negotiating lesser pay for an employee.[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-440) Viewers of the show tried to add the episode's mention of the page as a section of the actual Wikipedia article on negotiation, but this effort was prevented by other users on the article's talk page.[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-441)

"[My Number One Doctor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/My_Number_One_Doctor "My Number One Doctor")", a 2007 episode of the television show _[Scrubs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scrubs\_(TV\_series) "Scrubs (TV series)")_, played on the perception that Wikipedia is an unreliable reference tool with a scene in which [Perry Cox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perry_Cox "Perry Cox") reacts to a patient who says that a Wikipedia article indicates that the [raw food diet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raw_food_diet "Raw food diet") reverses the effects of [bone cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bone_cancer "Bone cancer") by retorting that the same editor who wrote that article also wrote the [_Battlestar Galactica_ episode guide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Battlestar_Galactica_(2004_TV_series)_episodes "List of Battlestar Galactica (2004 TV series) episodes").[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-Bakken_one_doctor_1-442)

In 2008, the comedy website _[CollegeHumor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CollegeHumor "CollegeHumor")_ produced a video sketch named "Professor Wikipedia", in which the fictitious Professor Wikipedia instructs a class with a medley of unverifiable and occasionally absurd statements.[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-443) The _[Dilbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dilbert "Dilbert")_ comic strip from May 8, 2009, features a character supporting an improbable claim by saying "Give me ten minutes and then check Wikipedia."[[317]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-444) In July 2009, [BBC Radio 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC_Radio_4 "BBC Radio 4") broadcast a comedy series called _[Bigipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bigipedia "Bigipedia")_, which was set on a website which was a parody of Wikipedia.[[318]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-445) Some of the sketches were directly inspired by Wikipedia and its articles.[[319]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-446)

On August 23, 2013, the _[New Yorker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_New\_Yorker "The New Yorker")_ website published a cartoon with this caption: "Dammit, Manning, have you considered the pronoun war that this is going to start on your Wikipedia page?"[[320]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-447) The cartoon referred to [Chelsea Elizabeth Manning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chelsea_Manning "Chelsea Manning") (born Bradley Edward Manning), an American activist, politician, and former United States Army soldier who had recently [come out](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coming_out "Coming out") as a [trans woman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trans_woman "Trans woman").[[321]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-448)

In June 2024, [nature.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nature.com "Nature.com") published a fictional Wikipedia [Talk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Talk_pages "Help:Talk pages") page under the title "Plastic-eating fungus caused doomsday" by Emma Burnett. The Talk page concerned a fictional article describing the unintended consequences of the release of a plastic-eating fungus to clean up an oil spill. The article contained Talk page topics found on Wikipedia, like discussions of changes in the articles [priority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Version_1.0_Editorial_Team/Release_Version_Criteria#Priority_of_topic "Wikipedia:Version 1.0 Editorial Team/Release Version Criteria") level.[[322]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-449)

### Publishing

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/WikiMedia_DC_2013_Annual_Meeting_08.JPG/250px-WikiMedia_DC_2013_Annual_Meeting_08.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:WikiMedia_DC_2013_Annual_Meeting_08.JPG)

A group of Wikimedians of the [Wikimedia DC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikimedia_DC "Wikimedia DC") chapter at the 2013 DC Wikimedia annual meeting standing in front of the _Encyclopædia Britannica_ _(back left)_ at the US National Archives

The most obvious economic effect of Wikipedia has been the death of commercial encyclopedias, especially printed versions like _[Encyclopædia Britannica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia\_Britannica "Encyclopædia Britannica")_, which were unable to compete with a product that is essentially free.[[323]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-450)[[324]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-451)[[325]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-FT_effect_on_traditional_media-452)[Nicholas Carr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicholas_G._Carr "Nicholas G. Carr")'s 2005 essay "The amorality of [Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0 "Web 2.0")" criticizes websites with [user-generated content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User-generated_content "User-generated content") (like Wikipedia) for possibly leading to professional (and, in his view, superior) content producers' going out of business, because "free trumps quality all the time". Carr wrote, "Implicit in the ecstatic visions of Web 2.0 is the [hegemony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hegemony "Hegemony") of the amateur. I for one can't imagine anything more frightening."[[326]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-RType_WP_traditional_media_effect_1-453) Others dispute the notion that Wikipedia, or similar efforts, will entirely displace traditional publications. [Chris Anderson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chris_Anderson_(writer) "Chris Anderson (writer)"), the former editor-in-chief of _[Wired](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wired\_(magazine) "Wired (magazine)")_, wrote in _[Nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nature\_(journal) "Nature (journal)")_ that the "[wisdom of crowds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wisdom_of_the_crowd "Wisdom of the crowd")" approach of Wikipedia will not displace top [scientific journals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_journal "Scientific journal") with rigorous [peer review](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer_review "Peer review") processes.[[327]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-nature.com_crowds_wisdom-454)

Wikipedia's influence on the biography publishing business has been a concern for some. Book publishing data tracker Nielsen [BookScan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BookScan "BookScan") stated in 2013 that biography sales were dropping "far more sharply".[[328]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-:22-455)[Kathryn Hughes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kathryn_Hughes "Kathryn Hughes"), professor of [life writing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_writing "Life writing") at the [University of East Anglia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_East_Anglia "University of East Anglia") and author of two biographies wrote, "The worry is that, if you can get all that information from Wikipedia, what's left for biography?"[[328]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-:22-455)

### Research use

Wikipedia has been widely used as a [corpus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Text_corpus "Text corpus") for linguistic research in [computational linguistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_linguistics "Computational linguistics"), [information retrieval](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_retrieval "Information retrieval") and [natural language processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_language_processing "Natural language processing").[[329]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-456)[[330]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-457) In particular, it commonly serves as a target knowledge base for the [entity linking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entity_linking "Entity linking") problem, which is then called "wikification",[[331]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-wikify-458) and to the related problem of [word-sense disambiguation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word-sense_disambiguation "Word-sense disambiguation").[[332]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-milne_witten_WP_usage_1-459) Methods similar to wikification can in turn be used to find "missing" links in Wikipedia.[[333]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-discovering_missing_WP_links_1-460)

In 2015, French researchers José Lages of the [University of Franche-Comté](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Franche-Comt%C3%A9 "University of Franche-Comté") in [Besançon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Besan%C3%A7on "Besançon") and Dima Shepelyansky of [Paul Sabatier University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Sabatier_University "Paul Sabatier University") in [Toulouse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toulouse "Toulouse") published a global university ranking based on Wikipedia scholarly citations.[[334]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-mitmining-461)[[335]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-harvardisonlymarmow-462)[[336]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-wikipediarankingtimesworldunifranche-463) They used [PageRank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PageRank "PageRank"), [CheiRank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CheiRank "CheiRank") and similar algorithms "followed by the number of appearances in the 24 different language editions of Wikipedia (descending order) and the century in which they were founded (ascending order)".[[336]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-wikipediarankingtimesworldunifranche-463)[[337]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-464) The study was updated in 2019.[[338]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-465)

In December 2015, [John Julius Norwich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Julius_Norwich "John Julius Norwich") stated, in a letter published in _[The Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Times "The Times")_ newspaper, that as a historian he resorted to Wikipedia "at least a dozen times a day", and had "never caught it out". He described it as "a work of reference as useful as any in existence", with so wide a range that it is almost impossible to find a person, place, or thing that it has left uncovered and that he could never have written his last two books without it.[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-466)

A 2017 [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT") study suggests that words used in Wikipedia articles end up in scientific publications.[[340]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-467) Studies related to Wikipedia have been using [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning") and [artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence "Artificial intelligence")[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-NYT-20230718-429) to support various operations. One of the most important areas is the automatic detection of vandalism[[341]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-468)[[342]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-469) and [data quality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_quality "Data quality") assessment in Wikipedia.[[343]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-470)[[344]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-471)

In February 2022, [civil servants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_Service_(United_Kingdom) "Civil Service (United Kingdom)") from the UK's [Housing, Communities and Local Government Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Housing,_Communities_and_Local_Government_Committee "Housing, Communities and Local Government Committee") were found to have used Wikipedia for research after journalists at _[The Independent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Independent "The Independent")_ noted that parts of the [document](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_paper#In_government "White paper") had been lifted directly from Wikipedia articles on [Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantinople "Constantinople") and the [list of largest cities throughout history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_cities_throughout_history "List of largest cities throughout history").[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-472)

Several interactive multimedia encyclopedias incorporating entries written by the public existed long before Wikipedia was founded. The first of these was the 1986 [BBC Domesday Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC_Domesday_Project "BBC Domesday Project"), which included text (entered on [BBC Micro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC_Micro "BBC Micro") computers) and photographs from more than a million contributors in the UK, and covered the geography, art, and culture of the UK. This was the first interactive multimedia encyclopedia (and was also the first major multimedia document connected through internal links), with the majority of articles being accessible through an interactive map of the UK. The user interface and part of the content of the Domesday Project were emulated on a website until 2008.[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-Domesday_Project-473)

Several free-content, collaborative encyclopedias were created around the same period as Wikipedia (e.g. [Everything2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Everything2 "Everything2")),[[347]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-474) with many later being merged into the project (e.g. [GNE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GNE_(encyclopedia) "GNE (encyclopedia)")).[[W 121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-475) One of the most successful early online encyclopedias incorporating entries by the public was [h2g2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H2g2 "H2g2"), which was created by [Douglas Adams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Adams "Douglas Adams") in 1999. The h2g2 encyclopedia is relatively lighthearted, focusing on articles which are both witty and informative.[[348]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-476)

Subsequent collaborative [knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_base "Knowledge base") websites have drawn inspiration from Wikipedia. Others use more traditional [peer review](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer_review "Peer review"), such as _[Encyclopedia of Life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopedia\_of\_Life "Encyclopedia of Life")_ and the online wiki encyclopedias _[Scholarpedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scholarpedia "Scholarpedia")_ and [Citizendium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citizendium "Citizendium").[[349]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-477)[[350]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-478) The latter was started by Sanger in an attempt to create a reliable alternative to Wikipedia.[[351]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-Orlowski18-479)[[352]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_note-JayLyman-480)

34.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia#cite_ref-158)**[Sanger, Larry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_Sanger "Larry Sanger") (December 31, 2004). ["Why Wikipedia Must Jettison Its Anti-Elitism"](https://web.archive.org/web/20211101011352/http://www.kuro5hin.org/story/2004/12/30/142458/25). _[Kuro5hin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuro5hin "Kuro5hin"), Op–Ed_. Archived from [the original](https://www.kuro5hin.org/story/2004/12/30/142458/25) on November 1, 2021. Retrieved March 26, 2021. There is a certain mindset associated with unmoderated Usenet groups [...] that infects the collectively-managed Wikipedia project: if you react strongly to trolling, that reflects poorly on you, not (necessarily) on the troll. If you [...] demand that something be done about constant disruption by trollish behavior, the other listmembers will cry "censorship", attack you, and even come to the defense of the troll. [...] The root problem: anti-elitism, or lack of respect for expertise. There is a deeper problem [...] which explains both of the above-elaborated problems. Namely, as a community, Wikipedia lacks the habit or tradition of respect for expertise. As a community, far from being elitist, it is anti-elitist (which, in this context, means that expertise is not accorded any special respect, and snubs and disrespect of expertise are tolerated). This is one of my failures: a policy that I attempted to institute in Wikipedia's first year, but for which I did not muster adequate support, was the policy of respecting and deferring politely to experts. (Those who were there will, I hope, remember that I tried very hard.)
